Hold on to your seats, because things are only going to accelerate from here." And he was really right. And I think you could have said that again in 2017; you could have said it again in 2020. I think you can really say that right now. Like, it has just continued to logarithmically accelerate, and we're going through something now where we're at the front end of AI. But I just think we're going to have to learn to live with this. Well, I don't know that, from an evolutionary standpoint, our brains have been able to deal with things like social media and some of the other changes. It's going to take a lot of work for us to just prepare ourselves as a species, and even mentally, how to digest all of this information. And Brad Gerstner talks about this book, *Essentialism*... Essentialism, and I think we also have to adopt this idea of essentialism because you can't do it all. You have to figure out the things that are most important and most relevant to what you do. I was going to ask, like, imagining five years hence, or, you know, pick your period of time, when it seems that we're destined for a world where no human will be anywhere near as good at investing—you know, pattern recognition, synthesis of data, analysis, that sort of stuff. Like, you could argue that's already here, if you had the right pipeline set up and the right data. What will be the role, do you think, of capital allocators in that kind of environment? Where, I think about, like, your early letters that you wrote to some of these companies, where you really had to pick your way through datasets and information, and do some gumshoe-type work to find the data, and that was—you could earn a ton of alpha that way. It seems like we're destined for a world where, like, if something is accessible by a computer, that's over. So, what will be the remainder job of the capital allocator, do you think? I honestly, I have no idea. I don't even know what it's going to look like in six months or a year from now. I think we're okay for the next couple of years. I think companies will need to raise money, the securities that are created from that will need to change hands somehow, people want to save, people want to invest, people need to borrow. And I think you'll always need some human interface in the middle of that. But how that's going to... like, I think it's highly unlikely that there will be a completely, you know, AI-managed capital system. I talk a lot about investment books. I think there's so much wisdom in books for investing, like, one of my favorite books, *Reminiscences of a Stock Operator*. The guy who wrote it quotes—I think it's from Ecclesiastes, which says there's nothing new under the sun. And human nature... The question is, will AI take human nature and the flaws in human emotion out of the investment process? Because there is a lot of... or, might the AIs even adopt some of that under the name of, you know, risk management or managing downside or whatever? I mean, in theory I guess it could take it out, but if you go back to the... it will test the theory that there's nothing new under the sun. And the thing that doesn't change is, yes, hysterias, bubbles, panics, and just the extremes of human nature, both optimistically and pessimistically. I mean, think about just this year, why is the SOX up so much? Well, it's up so much because all the evidence pointed to the fundamentals in semiconductors, semi cap equipment, memory, everything around it being super strong. So, what happened? Expectations were too high. And, in the same way that Nvidia, after Q1 three years ago, had this monster quarter, people piled on, it kept going up. You had a couple of quarters in a row where it put up solid numbers, then shockingly good numbers, and then the stock tanked. And then the whole sector went down, and I think people were just scratching their heads saying, "Why is everything... why do the numbers look so good, and numbers keep going up, and stock prices keep going down?" And then, the same thing happened with Micron. It had a phenomenal quarter, up 80%, you know, way ahead of expectations. The stock went up a little bit because expectations were too high, and then went down. Now, that happens a lot. It happened to Meta a couple of years ago; they put up a good quarter, the stock went up, but it was like Wile E. Coyote—there was no one else to buy the stock, and it tanked. And, you know, so those things happen. And, I think that's what, you know, maybe that's where the human element comes in to understand and to be able to make those tough trading decisions when fundamentals are going one way and stock prices are going the other way, and to be able to take the pain of losses in the short run. I think you have enough... the advantage that someone like me—and someone like me being a fundamental investor who doesn't make trading decisions based on computers—has is that there are still a lot of market irregularities caused by some very good strategies, but collectively they create these anomalies. So, you have quants and CTAs, you have pods, and let's talk about them for a second. They have a great strategy for them and their investors, but it causes some unusual behaviors because fundamental investors believe that when, you know, as Warren Buffett would say, if a stock goes down, you celebrate it because it's a chance to buy more at a better price. They have risk metrics which have forced selling on the way down, so they do the opposite. It might be rational for their business model, but it's not rational for long-term investors. So, you have a lot of these things that will continue to create opportunities, I think, for fundamental investors. So, so interesting to imagine where the source of potential outsized returns are human behavior, which, as you said, maybe that changes how much that impacts prices, and structural things like these. And, absent those two, maybe it would be a less exciting market or something like that. Yeah. But then, you know, you'll have corporate transactions that will create opportunities. It feels like there's always something: there's failures, there's credit cycles, there's bankruptcies. It's hard to imagine the computers sitting on a creditors' committee and working through the capital structure and being able to transact. If you think about a continuum of public securities and private equity, the AI will not do private equity. You'll always need people to do deals. I think you will. And then you have the kind of the stuff in between that requires a lot of negotiation and human interaction and high touch—you know, private credit, or, like I said before, working through a restructuring. So, you'll probably always need human beings to do that part of the investment business. It seems like we're entering a time, as well, where governance becomes incredibly important, especially in companies—we saw this play out with OpenAI—in the public. Where the governance structure and the people on a board matter, can matter tremendously, to the outcome. This has been an area where you've spent a huge amount of time thinking, writing, investing—you know, a lot of your great successes have come in and around this topic. I want to ask all about governance. When did you first get interested in it, like, what sparked your... yeah, where did it come from? Well, actually, my dad, who was a securities lawyer, was an expert on corporate governance and wrote books about it, so I always heard about it at home. He's actually the first person I'd ever heard of who actually talked about corporate responsibility. He was on the board of Mattel, and later Williams-Sonoma. He would go visit these factories where they were sourcing their materials from and wanted to make sure that they were ethically sourced and the workers were treated well at Mattel or Williams-Sonoma. So, he was really ahead of his time on that stuff. What was he like as a person? My dad was incredibly funny, warm, irreverent, and really smart. He was the only son of two immigrants from Europe. My grandmother, his mother, came from Poland in 1914. His father came from Romania in like 1898. Incidentally, his mother's youngest sister ended up... she was the first of that family that was born in this country, and her younger sister founded Mattel Toys. He was a self-made person, went to UCLA, did well, went to Harvard Law School, and spent most of his career at one law firm. I'm curious for you to sum up, if you think about everything you've done in governance, what good and bad governance are, just at the highest possible level. How do you think about it? We have an incredible system. Let's start with that. There's something beautiful about the American capitalist system and the aspect of it that creates boards of directors that ultimately have a role within both capitalism and a democratic system where the board is responsible for and answerable to the shareholders, and is responsible for accountability for the management, setting strategy, and other key financial decisions. So, we have a great system in place. Where the shortcomings within governance happen is when the board members lose sight of what their duties are as fiduciaries, or the composition of the board is such that they aren't really equipped to carry out that duty because there is a lack of deep knowledge, or intellectual or talent diversity on the board, or they are thinking excessively about things other than their duty to shareholders. Not to say that the board doesn't have other responsibilities, but I think ultimately they should feed into creating shareholder value. If you go back to both Milton Friedman and things that Warren Buffett have said, of course, boards care about the communities that they serve, the products, the employees, proper conduct, etc. All those things are very important and they are not inconsistent with creating shareholder value. In fact, it's part of creating shareholder value. But, I think, especially a few years ago, I think it was the Business Roundtable, said, "We're no longer going to say that a board's responsibility is primarily to drive shareholder value; it's this thing, and that thing, and the other thing." Well, I think it was a distraction from what their real duty is and it didn't recognize how all these things, of course, go together. You know, so the bad governance I've seen is when, from a number of things, one, they let their loyalty or relationship to a CEO, who's not up to the job, overshadow their duty to shareholders. That's probably the main thing because the CEO... you know, it's very important to understand, the boards don't run the companies. The board's responsibility, in a well-functioning company, the board is strategic, not tactical, so they should be really focusing on those things. But if the company isn't allocating capital well, or isn't holding the management team responsible, or there's some very obvious things that should be done differently, that's when we can come in. Most of the time in our experiences, we've been able to work with existing boards in kind of redirecting them and offering solutions, or sometimes we don't even have to get on the board to get those things done. Kind of you hear about the extreme cases where we have to, or try to shake things up, where we actually, you know, we have to... our presence is needed on the board to do that. What have you learned about the power of writing in investing? You're obviously extremely well known for some letters you've written to chairs of the board, things like this, throughout your entire career. And, I just know you're a writer. You've used writing in a lot of different ways. What is great writing to you, and how do you use it well? Great writing is really about clear thinking and organizing your thoughts, communicating them to people in a clear way, to get a desired outcome. I would say that, you know, you can also use writing to influence and, in our case, it's been helpful in getting the attention of other shareholders, sometimes the board itself, shaking them up a little bit, getting media attention focused on a board. I think about, you know, activism—you've got a few different levers: you can have, obviously, a financial lever, by... we can make a bid for the company; you have legal levers, proxy contests, litigation, information requests, etc. Social pressure is actually a very effective way, and I think the best way to put social pressure on a company is through writing and PR efforts around the company. If I look across all the different activism that you've done, it seems like maybe there's an interesting theme of you doing activism on places, or people, or companies that hold themselves out as quite high status, but then they're not living up to it. Whether that's Sotheby's or some of the Japanese conglomerates, or just like certain CEOs and their family members on the board, things like this. There's like a status component that would be deserved if being earned on an ongoing basis, but maybe the company wasn't. And you saw that gap as an opportunity. Do you think that's a thing? Do I see a correct pattern? That's interesting. That certainly was the case in Sotheby's. Can you tell that story? That's such a good story. Such an interesting one. I'll come back to the story about Sotheby's in a second. But I think all board members often feel a sense of status for being on these boards. And, I think that in itself needs to be dispelled because I think if you're on a board because you are either getting status or you're getting income, and that's your primary reason for doing it, not for representing shareholders, then that's where we come in to try to disintermediate that and take away some of the status or increase the cost of doing that. Sotheby's I thought was just an interesting company. It was a pretty small company, and a small target for us. That's actually a very good example of what you're talking about because although it was a public company, it wasn't really run for the shareholders. It was run because I think people did feel like it was a high-status business and had been mismanaged, didn't really recover from an antitrust violation that spilled into kind of criminal charges brought against the company and some of the individuals. And, the business itself was a good business, but just run unbelievably unprofitably, and, you know, the company had been around since the 1700s, and some of the business practices had not really been updated since that time. So, we took a position; we bought 9.9% of the company. We went after the board, but really just wanted them to implement some basic business practices that we thought would be better. We didn't think the CEO that was there had sort of come up... he didn't have a particularly deep knowledge of art. He came—I think he came out of the rug division—and didn't really have deep relationships with the collectors, either. So, we came in and gave him a shot for a year, and then, I think the board came to realize he wasn't the right guy. We brought in a guy named Tad Smith, who was terrific, from MSG, cleaned up the operations, improved the technology, and then they sold the company—you know, good result. Yeah, we had a good result. Do you think that there's a lot of that lurking out there today? Like, if one wanted to start a career and only do that—go find companies where it was kind of mediocre or bad management, where if it was good management, the company would do way, way, way better? Do you think there's still a lot of that out there, or... There's probably some of that in the kind of sub-two billion dollar market cap space, and not necessarily even bad management, but maybe B+ management, not optimizing. What we're finding is that it's almost like a negative selection process. Like, we'd much rather invest in a great company with awesome management that's doing all the right things, and cheer them on, than kind of find something that but for, you know, mismanagement, the company would be worth a lot more. Because what you find is that if the things that you've identified are badly run, then there's probably, you know, 10 times more things that are badly run. It just gets to be sort of a morass. And one of the interesting things about Third Point is that, if I understand it correctly, it's something like 60% credit, which I think would surprise a lot of people, of just, like, the total assets. And also, when you started, I think you started with no institutional investors—it was individuals, families, things like this. And it seems like you sort of have felt your way into the strategy. Like, the strategy as it exists today probably wouldn't go on a clean PowerPoint deck if you were a new firm doing this from scratch; it would be hard to pitch what you have now. And I'm really interested by that—by a firm which has evolved into its model, and it's 60% credit today and it's a blend of other things. And I'd love you to just talk about that evolution and why it is, and the value of that evolution versus, like, predicting, "Okay, here's exactly what we're going to do." Yeah. Well, first of all, Third Point is a whole collection of businesses. My main focus is on the hedge fund strategy, which started at three million dollars; it's now about nine billion dollars today. That fund itself is about 20%... it's about 30% credit in total. The rest of the portfolio, I mean, it moves all over the place. It is primarily equities, and I think our equity book is generically around 110 long by 30 or 40 short. But that can be all over the place. I mean, I think going into the war, we really dialed back our risk, and we were, I think for the first time since 2009, we did have more credit exposure than equity. But we very quickly have taken that back up. But we are... for the hedge fund, we're still more equity than credit. But across the fund, we have a CLO business with about seven billion dollars in it. Within Third Point itself, we have about 30%—which would make it close to three billion dollars—in structured credit and corporate credit, but that's in the hedge fund. And then we have an insurance company that we manage about a billion dollars in credit for, and we have a couple billion dollars in asbestos liabilities that we manage in its own pool. And, we just started a private credit business, which is small. What's the thread that unites all those things? Like asbestos liability, and private credit, and corporates? It seems kind of all over the place. Well, we haven't even talked about the fact that we have a venture capital business. I've worked in venture capital, I've worked in risk arbitrage, I've worked in credit, I've worked, you know, in equities. And, I think having a view of value, and thinking about valuing enterprises, whether they are earlier stage, mid-stage, or mature businesses, and looking to invest in whatever the fulcrum security is in that enterprise. Obviously, for an early-stage company, the only fulcrum is the equity. What does that mean, fulcrum, just to define that? The one that's going to have the best risk-reward. It's usually used in terms of companies that have, you know, debt and equity. It's more in terms of, like, companies that are going through sort of like a restructuring of some sort—like, do you want to be in the equity? Do you want to be in the junior debt? Do you want to be in the senior debt? Credit Suisse, when it was going through its troubles and being bought by UBS, you could invest in the preferred shares, you could invest in the holdco paper, or you could invest in the opco paper, which was most senior within the capital structure. The fulcrum there was actually the holdco paper. That had the most upside, but the opco paper also did well. The pref was wiped out, so that was, you know, the wrong place to be. There are always different interesting places within the capital structure to play, and having a comprehensive view of these companies gives you a great vantage point to make alpha-generating investment decisions. Let me give you two examples. We had a workable, a good enough knowledge of Twitter and xAI to understand the equity value of both of those businesses. And, without making a decision whether or not we wanted to own equity in xAI, and having not participated in the Twitter deal, there were two financing transactions that came out: one for xAI and another one for Twitter. The Twitter debt was a resale of the financing debt that was offered when Elon bought the company. Morgan Stanley sat on it for a while; it was deep underwater. When it got close to par, they decided to sell it. Most credit investors were really scared and nervous to buy that, even though it was like 96 or 97 cents on the dollar, and it was yielding around a 12% yield. We were comfortable enough with the underlying value of the business and with the fundamentals, that we made that at that time our largest credit position. And then when xAI did a debt financing, very few credit people wanted to play in that one because there was no cash flow, 2 billion dollars in revenues, and a 20 billion dollar enterprise value. But we were very comfortable that this was a real business. We looked at them as credit investors, but we also were able to bring in the resources of our private investing knowledge. Yeah, it's a fascinating example of the value of seeing across the whole ecosystem and being able to invest however you want. Which brings me to the question of, as I understand it, you're sort of still the single portfolio manager that sits on top of all these assets across all these different buckets. Like, ultimately you have to make the decision to buy or sell something. Let me just push back on that. I'm the portfolio manager of the hedge fund. But private credit, CLOs, structured credit, and our high-yield business all have their own PMs. I will come in if there's like an interesting opportunity—like we supervised both Twitter and xAI when I got involved—but I'm not even on the investment committees of those businesses. Yeah, okay. So to zoom in on just the hedge fund where you're the PM: you mentioned before, you know, you could be a great investor and not bother with tech, like Warren Buffett-style or something. And now today the market's whatever, 70% tech or, depending on how you measure it. What do you think of that complex of companies today—the sort of Amazon, Microsoft, Google, you know, big technology companies relative to the last 10 years of watching them and investing in them carefully? Like, what does the setup feel like to you today? Well, I think the setup's great. I mean, you can still buy Nvidia; maybe the multiple's slightly higher right now. I mean, there's such a catch-up trade in Nvidia at 15 times '27, 12 times '28, for the most dominant, fastest... I don't know if it's the fastest-growing, but very fast-growing company at its size. I looked through our whole semis, cap equipment, hyperscaler portfolio, and my instinct was going to be, "Okay, we've got to take profits here." But I looked at the valuations, I looked at their growth rates, and unless you are really draconian or negative, and you think that somehow the AI world is going to roll over in '31 or '32, I think it's some of the most attractive... yeah, I think it's the most attractive sector right now. It's where the bulk of our capital is invested. As a person that was born in the sort of value discipline and made a lot of money in that kind of investing, those people who tend to have not evolved as much would point to today and say, "Oh, it's just like another classic example of like a giant bubble in the making." Do you feel seeds of that at all? Are there aspects of the market that feel euphoric or strange or out of bounds? If you don't believe the capex numbers are going to yield a return, then you would have to believe that they're just flushing money down the toilet and that they're not going to get a return. Because the earnings are really strong, and the multiples on that... and they might say, "Yes, but the cash flow after capex is..." But these are companies also that are investing money off their balance sheets and are generating enormous amounts of cash. So, it's very different from the .com bubble, which we were short going into and had good numbers in those years. I just don't see... you don't have the valuation bubble now on those companies that you had back in those days. You look at Anthropic's revenue growth, and the adoption, and the usefulness of its products, and the anecdotes that you hear about the next-generation LLMs and what that's going to do—you can make a good argument that we're barely scratching the surface. I mean, there are so many layers of corporations that are just getting started. So, I'm in the optimist camp in terms of seeing this as something that's going to play out. I'd love you to tell the Sony story—the story of going to Japan and spending so much time there and everything that you learned. I think that's a really colorful, cool story. But it's also an excuse to ask about the rest of the world, and what you see going on there. I know you've been willing to invest anywhere, whereas the American companies get basically all the attention in markets these days. So, yeah, whichever order you want to go, you can tell the Sony story first as just an example of this, but I'm just curious how you think about everything outside the US. Israel's an interesting market. It's kind of a niche-ier market, and we had one of our top investments there that, despite the war, has been one of the best performing stocks in our portfolio. But in terms of the big markets, yeah, there's a lot going on in Korea, Taiwan, Japan. I'm probably more bullish on them, just in terms of a hunting ground to find great companies. The European markets are just tough right now, given the regulatory environment. They just have a different attitude about business and capitalism, and, you know, we're invested in a couple of businesses there—Rolls-Royce, ASM, ASML—but the companies that are in Europe dependent on the local economy are more challenged. Can you tell that Sony investment story and kind of everything you learned through that process? And what you did? Yeah. I mean, Sony was... at one point we owned 7% of Sony, and there's a list of companies that we at one point owned significant stakes in that, had I not sold, would be worth in the mid-to-upper single digits billions of dollars. So, I feel like I hear this all the time. Yeah, I mean, we all have that. We sort of took two runs at Sony. The first time we invested in it, it was basically a conglomerate. It had, obviously, the main Sony Studios, it had a semiconductor business, a life insurance business, and obviously it had all the consumer electronics. We advised them to separate these businesses—certainly, at a minimum, take out the insurance business which had no place in it—and we met with the management team, and we had a big deck that we went through. And, at the end of the meeting, we told them, in the interest of transparency, we shared our investment thesis with the New York Times. Andrew Ross Sorkin wrote the story. They went into a panic when we told them about that, and Andrew agreed to embargo the story until the Japanese market closed. The story came out, and they had pre-arranged for us to go on a tour of their kind of innovation center. But before we went to the innovation center, when we told them that there was going to be a story that came out—and this was Kaz Hirai—we had to tell him. "You told the New York Times?" I said, "Yeah, but just the New York Times, nobody else." He was like, "Oh, okay, just the New York Times." So, anyway, it was wild. We were walking around the innovation center and we're looking at our Blackberries at that time, and the story just went everywhere. Anyway, it ended up being a really good investment. They really pushed back on everything that we recommended until... yeah, it took them about five years, and I think one by one they've done many of the things. They broke out the semi business, and they partially spun out, or plan to spin out, their financial services business. The one thing I learned is that activism in Japan is really hard. I'll tell you what's interesting about activism in Japan: on one of our first trips, we met with the Prime Minister, and we met with his right-hand man, Sugasan. And, I told him I would write a paper explaining to him why activism was good for Japan as a country. And they had released something called the Three Arrows, which was about fiscal, monetary, and restructuring. And my suggestion was that they needed to include corporate governance, and in particular a focus on return on invested capital, as part of their Three Arrow strategy. And I came back to New York, I met with Larry Lindsey and Niall Ferguson, and we wrote a three-person paper—they did most of the writing, but we wrote a paper for AEI. It was then picked up as an editorial in the Wall Street Journal, and they then adopted that. It was pretty cool. But the government actually really wants the companies to do this. It's really the management teams that are more entrenched, because the shareholders and the government kind of want that, and you've really seen progress since we first went over there. They're breaking up some of these cross-shareholdings, they're penalizing companies that trade at discounts to book value, and there's a bunch of other things. So I think, you know, they're definitely moving in the right direction. If you think about all the investments that you've made, is there any that stands out as the one that taught you the most about how this world works? I know I've asked this question a couple of different ways, but I'm just always interested in these instructive, learning-by-doing experiences versus learning by reading. You can read all these amazing books—*The Outsiders* and Joel Greenblatt's book—and they're great, but it feels like part of investing is you have to get hit in the face or experience things to really imbibe the lessons. Is there any investment through your career that stands out as one like that? I think investing in Danaher has been the most instructive because it really is truly one of the best-run businesses. It was also one of the first experiences that I had investing in a super high-quality business that internalized some of the best practices of creating a corporate operating system. My partner then, Munib, and I actually went to Danaher and got them to boil down their five-day DBS, Danaher Business System training, into a one-day thing for us. It was really instructive. It was a really, really good investment for about four years. By observing them, by watching them kind of incrementally improve the business quality of the company—by shedding lower quality businesses, and buying higher return on capital, better quality, higher margin businesses to shift from general industrials into healthcare—I learned a ton. And then, the stock stopped working as an investment because of COVID. There were all kinds of irregularities, like surges in orders, and increases in inventory, and then a correction for that. All the benefits that they got from the surge in demand that was a tailwind became a headwind. And they still haven't really come out of it. It'll be interesting to see how they navigate AI in the next few years. We actually sold it, and recently, with the recent sell-off, have gotten back in, but in a small way. But I learned a lot about how really thoughtful businesses think philosophically and deeply about their operating system, about optimizing, motivating, and inspiring their team, and even the fact that they have a system in place. And it's also been incredibly instructive watching the diaspora of executives—Larry Culp was there, and there have been a lot of others, like the guy from Ingersoll Rand, which has been a well-run business. If you had to sum up that one day, what was it? Just some system for continuous improvement was the thing that you took away, or... Cult's a little bit too strong of a word to describe it, but they had a very strong corporate identity and culture. It's one thing to say, "We are a Kaizen company and we're dedicated to continual improvement," but what they have is a whole system of implementing improvement across the organization. And, you know, one of my big takeaways from that day—there were a lot of things that I learned that day, but one of the things I took away—is that they would hold people very accountable, and individually show when people were underperforming. But the interesting thing about it was that, because these things were all addressable and fixable, when they found someone that was underperforming, it was celebrated instead of shamed. The attitude was, "Look at all these things you're doing wrong; we can fix those." And they did. They do that over and over and over again, and they do it both in terms of operations and working capital. It was really cool to just walk around a place and see how everybody was on the same wavelength trying to accomplish that. Can you talk about the insurance business that you've built and the acquisitions that you've done? On the same point, everyone talks about investing and the stuff you're putting money into, but there is less discussion of where the money is coming from. Apollo certainly has done this with Athene; there's been a lot of cool innovation on the liability side. How have you thought about it, what have you done, and what's the story? We actually started an insurance company de novo in 2010. We were backed by myself, Kelso, and Pine Brook, and we started a Bermuda-based reinsurance company. The thesis when we started was that we have an executive that will do these reinsurance deals, and we will invest the float all in Third Point and in Treasuries—sort of like a barbell—so we can defer taxes and get leverage on our capital. Greenlight Re at the time was trading at 140% of book value. I thought this would be the future and we would just keep raising money for this vehicle. The problem was that the reinsurance business took a sharp turn for the worse. We had some good years at Third Point, but we were scrambling to offset the losses from the insurance company. About three years ago, I said, "Well, we had the right idea, but we had the wrong insurance vehicle. We were doing P&C insurance; we really should have just done plain vanilla annuities, basically." The problem with the annuity business, though, is it can't invest in the hedge fund; it can only do credit. Well, the good news is, we had had about five years where Third Point stopped investing in my hedge fund, but we started investing in structured credit, corporate credit, and other things that were either rated or more appropriate for an insurance company. We've done two things since then: we merged our reinsurance company into a closed-end fund that we had in the UK, Third Point Offshore Investors. We reincorporated that business from Guernsey to Cayman, and we repurposed it from a closed-end investor in my hedge fund into an insurance company. It still has some of the investment in the hedge fund, but it now owns our reinsurance company. That company will then be in a position to do more reinsurance deals and issue primary annuities. Third Point then manages the money in private credit, structured credit, whole loan mortgages, some direct lending in real estate, and some investment-grade corporate debt and private investment-grade. We also put the equity of that business in things like the junior tranches of structured financings, but we'll also be using it for growth equity investments. What's the hardest investment lesson that you've ever learned? I would have to say our investment in FTX. It looked great. The company was growing fast, we could verify it all on the blockchain, and we felt like we had some good company on the cap table with us—which we did. It just turned out that it wasn't what we thought it was. It was painful because, in general, one of the amazing things about our capitalist system is that venture-backed companies have this incredible ability to go out and raise capital for interesting ideas. I'd say most people are good actors with good intentions, and we've rarely had any kind of mishap. That doesn't mean that you don't do very careful due diligence. I will say that now, in our due diligence process, we definitely check bank balances and do the most basic due diligence that probably would have turned stuff up on this. But if left alone, or if he hadn't ended up being a crook or very sloppy, the venture investments he made would have... I mean, he is like the best venture investor of this era: Cursor, Anthropic, Solana, everything. Everything he did. The guy had a great nose for value. So that was probably the toughest. The mistake that we made within the last two years—we obviously were looking for and we've made some great short investments in, or we've taken some great shorts in, things that are being disrupted by AI. But where we've made the mistake is that we were controversial, but we thought we knew better. We thought AI wasn't really going to affect this part of the info services business, or that these guys had private proprietary information. That's where we've made some mistakes. I still think there could be a shakeout—there probably will be a shakeout there where there will be some phoenixes that rise from the ashes—but that's been kind of the investment lesson of the last year or so. How do you guide your team through this? You've got a bunch of extremely smart, ambitious, and I'm sure hungry analysts, investors, etc. What do you tell them going into this crazy uncertainty? Yeah, I mean, first of all, we all have to just start using it. The only way to get good at this is just to use it. We have people at different levels. I think we're probably more optimistic, or I should say maybe less pessimistic, that there's going to be some kind of apocalypse from AI. I think the differentiator for us is that we can always default into credit investing. When things get... You don't just electronically trade bonds. You have to have relationships with these firms. These are not markets that lend themselves to tourism. When the opportunities really come up, we're already there with the relationships and the understanding. What makes a great analyst today in any way that's distinctive from what made a great analyst 20 years ago or something like that? Like, how has that changed? If you picture the most talented analyst here right now, whoever that is, what are they like? Yeah, I think that the great analyst 20 years ago was someone who could build a model really fast and understand some really complicated restructuring. I'll use myself as the example of that ancient dinosaur of an analyst that used to be useful. When I was at Jefferies, Drexel went bankrupt. There was a thick disclosure statement, about three or four inches thick, on this bankruptcy of a company called Drexel Burnham, and this thing got passed around and nobody could crack the code on it. I was relatively new, and I knew I had to differentiate myself, so I just spent a whole weekend studying this thing. Ultimately, that was one of the best investments ever in the history of bankruptcies—the claims on Drexel—because people didn't understand the complexity of the different value pools and liquidations. The claims were overstated, the assets were understated, and it was super complicated. That was the kind of thing that differentiated the analyst of the '90s, when I did that, or the 2000s. I think now, it's somebody who's like a Gavin Baker type—a junior Gavin Baker—somebody who understands a company or understands an industry, and understands the nuances of a technology. Let's get away from technology for a minute. Casey's General Stores: why was this one of the best performing stocks? It looked like a tech stock. It was because they were not a convenience store chain; they were a pizza chain kind of masquerading as convenience stores. We had an analyst who went to Texas and ate pizza and saw that. So, that kind of an analyst today, I think, is way different. If you think about the next 10 years for yourself and everything going on in markets, it feels like we're all lucky to be alive to watch this era. What excites you the most about this next 10-year period as an investor? You're lucky you have all these resources, and you can invest in anything: credit, equity, whatever. And then, what worries you the most? The thing that worries me the most is just not having the time to do the things that I care about—you know, spending time with my family, or being able to go surfing, and reading books that I want to read. I'm not really worried about the business. I mean, we have a good process, good investment principles, and we invest in things with good value. The thing that excites me is just the things that have always excited me, that kind of keep me going. I mean, God, what an awesome opportunity to be able to incorporate everything that you can possibly know about the world that's relevant: to study industries, to study technology, to study consumer behavior, to look at the US economy, look at politics, and to travel to the Middle East, which I think is probably the most vibrant, interesting part of the world. I mean, who would have thought 20 years ago, or even three years ago, that Bahrain, the Emirates, Saudi, Morocco, and Azerbaijan would be better allies to the US than NATO? It's crazy. And who would have thought that their growth rates would way exceed theirs, or that their growth and their embracing of technology would be so advanced? That's the stuff that keeps me going—just being able to incorporate all these different things, and also to form relationships with people that are doing interesting things, like Jeremy O'Brien, who founded PsiQuantum to work on quantum computing, or Elad Gil, or the CEOs of the companies that we invest in and get to know, like Mitch Rales from Danaher. It's so cool to finally hear about it directly and do this with you. When I do this, I ask the same traditional closing question of everyone: What's the kindest thing that anyone's ever done for you? Gosh, you know, I thought about that because I knew the question was coming, and I had a lot of different ways to answer it. But before I answer that, I do want to talk about the importance of kindness. I know you know it's important, because you ask this question every time you interview someone. It's important on many levels because, I think, if you kind of elevate kindness as a characteristic that you want to elevate in your hierarchy of things that you want to be—honest, and truthful, and smart, clever, innovative, whatever—kindness is very important. I think it goes with forming deep relationships with people. Sometimes it will, and sometimes it won't. Sometimes you just connect with someone who thinks you're a better person for its own sake. But anyway, I came down to my friend Carter. When I was, let's just say, in between jobs, before I started at Jefferies, I had about a six- or nine-month period where I wasn't working, and he let me sleep on his couch. Then, when I got my job at Jefferies, I suggested to him a bunch of different distressed debt situations, and he trusted me with a few hundred thousand dollars of his money. That turned into well over a million dollars, and he then rolled that into my fund, which really enabled me to get my business started. So, that was one of the kindest things. Chief Economists Outlook: counting the cost of the Hormuz crisis, with Maersk's Ilaria Maselli

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·41:08·728 views·2026-05-28
Ilaria MaselliHead of Macro & Market Insights, Maersk
TL;DRThe ongoing closure of the Strait of Hormuz has triggered one of the most severe energy and trade crises in modern capitalism, with World Economic Forum economists warning that a prolonged shutdown into the second half of 2026 will inflict an…
economic shock equivalent to...

TL;DR

The ongoing closure of the Strait of Hormuz has triggered one of the most severe energy and trade crises in modern capitalism, with World Economic Forum economists warning that a prolonged shutdown into the second half of 2026 will inflict an economic shock equivalent to nearly 84% of the impact of the COVID-19 pandemic.

The Scale of the Hormuz Chokepoint Crisis

The Strait of Hormuz—the vital artery for global fossil fuel trade—has been effectively closed since February 28, 2026, causing unprecedented disruptions to international commerce.

  • Collapse in Maritime Traffic:
    • Daily Transit Deficit: In normal times, approximately 120 commercial vessels crossed the strait daily. This volume has collapsed to a mere handful (fewer than 10 per day on average, with many days seeing zero transits).
    • Severe Energy Gap: Prior to the crisis, the strait carried 25% of global seaborne oil trade and 20% of global liquefied natural gas (LNG) exports. Currently, approximately 6 million barrels of oil per day are missing from the global market in Q2 2026.
  • Persistent Geopolitical and Physical Barriers:
    • Fragile Ceasefires: Ceasefires brokered between the US and Iran (April 8) and Israel and Lebanon (April 16) have reduced outright hostilities but failed to restore safe passage.
    • Physical Hazards: Iran has deployed sea mines in the strait. According to a US Department of Defense report to Congress, fully de-mining the channel could take up to six months.
    • Sanctions and Blockades: Shipowners face severe legal and financial risks. Iranian authorities demand transit-related payments that violate Western sanctions, while the US blockade under Central Command (Centcom) had already diverted 89 commercial vessels and disabled four as of May 19.

Economic Fallout and the "COVID Benchmark"

According to the World Economic Forum's May 2026 *Chief Economists Outlook*, a survey of global public and private sector economists reveals a sharp deterioration in macroeconomic confidence compared to January 2026.

  • Plunging Growth Outlook:
    • Pessimistic Consensus: 89% of chief economists now expect global economic growth to weaken over the coming year, a massive surge from the 53% who expected weakening in January.
    • Severe Weakening: More than 20% of economists expect global growth to be "severely weakened," while only 8% expect any economic strengthening (down from 20% in January).
  • The Pandemic Benchmark: Economists rated the relative economic severity of the Hormuz crisis against the COVID-19 pandemic (set at a benchmark of 100):
    • 2025 Tariff Turmoil: Scored 40 on the scale.
    • Hormuz Reopening by June 2026: Projected to score 72.
    • Hormuz Closure Extending into H2 2026: Projected to score 83.5, meaning the prolonged shutdown would inflict nearly 84% of the economic damage of the pandemic.
  • Systemic Inflationary Pressure:
    • Core Inflation Risks: Over 90% of chief economists expect increased inflation. High energy prices are quickly bleeding into core inflation, driving up the costs of manufacturing, chemicals, and transportation.
    • Monetary Policy Pivot: The anticipated stabilization and reduction of central bank interest rates are now off the table. Rates are expected to remain higher for longer, eroding household disposable income and stalling business investments.

Supply Chain Paralysis and the Lack of Alternatives

Unlike other major shipping chokepoints, the geography of the Persian Gulf offers no realistic maritime alternatives to the Strait of Hormuz.

  • No Rerouting Options:
    • Comparison to Bab-el-Mandeb: Since early 2024, Houthi attacks have forced container ships to bypass the Red Sea and circumnavigate Africa. While this added 20 days to voyages, the industry adapted. Hormuz, by contrast, has no physical maritime bypass.
    • Logistical Inefficiencies: Shipping firms are utilizing "land bridges" by unloading goods at ports outside the Gulf (such as Jeddah in Saudi Arabia, Salalah in Oman, or Khor Fakkan in the UAE) and trucking them overland. However, a single 20,000-container vessel requires 20,000 individual trucks, making this approach highly inefficient and impossible to scale.
  • Severe Trade Contraction:
    • Import Slump: In March 2026, containerized imports into the Middle East contracted by 40% year-on-year, starving major regional hubs like Dubai’s Jebel Ali and Qatar's Doha of fast-moving consumer goods and automotive parts.
    • Export Shock: Regional containerized exports contracted by 46% year-on-year in March. Critical global supply chains are being starved of primary plastics, aluminum, chemicals, fertilizers, and helium (a critical component for artificial intelligence hardware).

The Broader Global Geopolitical Picture

The Hormuz crisis occurs during a highly volatile transition phase for the global economic order, where supply chains have shifted from economic efficiency tools to instruments of foreign and security policy.

  • Compounding Global Chokepoints:
    • Panama Canal Vulnerability: Climate change and El Niño patterns threaten the Panama Canal with low water levels (similar to 2023), presenting compounding risks if multiple straits experience simultaneous disruptions.
  • Trade Policy Volatility:
    • US Tariff Shifts: A temporary 10% tariff on all US imports (following a Supreme Court ruling) is set to expire in July 2026, leaving businesses highly uncertain about incoming sector-specific replacements.
    • USMCA Negotiations: The upcoming renegotiation of the US-Mexico-Canada Agreement reveals diverging strategies, with Mexico aligning closely with US trade policies while Canada takes a more confrontational stance.
    • Contradictory Trade Signals: While Western capitals are tightening protectionist measures against strong Chinese exports, historic and previously stalled bilateral trade deals—such as EU-Mercosur and EU-India—have unexpectedly been signed.
  • Three Competing Economic Forces: Economists are closely watching the balance between the negative economic drag of the Hormuz energy shock, the offsetting growth fueled by the AI investment boom in the US and East Asia (Korea and Taiwan), and the baseline stabilization provided by massive fiscal deficits (6% of GDP in the US and nearly 3% in the Eurozone).

Notable Quotes

"It is around 6 million barrels per day that are missing, which is probably one of the biggest crises we have witnessed in the memory of capitalism." — Ilaria Maselli, Head of Macro & Market Insights, Maersk
"Every day matters at this point, and the longer the conflict continues, the more we will downgrade the expectations for growth and increase the expectations for inflation." — Ilaria Maselli, Head of Macro & Market Insights, Maersk
"In the world we live in today, I think the view around trade and supply chains has changed. For a long time, they were mostly a tool for economic growth, and now they're rather a tool for foreign policy and security policy." — Ilaria Maselli, Head of Macro & Market Insights, Maersk

Speaking of inflation, also in the Chief Economists Outlook, overwhelmingly, more than 90%, well over 90% of the chief economists expect increased inflationary pressure over the coming year as a result of this supply shock for the ages. Are you in agreement with that? And maybe help our listeners understand a little bit why it is that economists and a lot of other people get very nervous when the topic of inflation and an intense inflationary cycle comes up? Energy prices, of course, play a very important role in inflation figures, and we have seen them rising actually very fast. Already, if you follow a bit Eurozone inflation numbers, or the US CPI, then you can see that there's been a change very fast already in March and April figures—inflation has gone up. And the channel for that is the higher energy cost. Oil and gas are more expensive now than they were prior; they were pretty low actually going into this war. That's the first channel. Then, you can think that the longer energy prices remain high, then the more they will go, not only from affecting inflation through the energy cost, but they also will go into core inflation. Energy is an input in a lot of processes, and so everything all of a sudden becomes more expensive: producing goods, transportation. So, other items of the global total inflation number start to become affected. This, of course, first and foremost, is like a big tax. It just makes everyone poorer. Higher prices mean that households will have a lower disposable income because wages don't adjust immediately, and we don't know if they will adjust in the future either. So, that, that is the first, the very first concern. Disposable income of households will be affected by higher energy prices. Then, the other channel of concern is inflation expectations. So, the fact that we have higher inflation in April—and I'm sure we will have in May once we get the figure—does it mean that I'm changing my expectations about long-term inflation? And this is very important for central banks who are in charge of keeping an anchor around inflation expectations. So, central banks are definitely looking at core inflation especially; they are definitely looking at how wages adjust to that and how long, basically, this shock will impact inflation. And inflation is a key target, of course, of central banks, and the main target for some central banks like the European Central Bank. There will be a lot of discussions around: okay, in this new environment where there is potentially less growth and more inflation, what is the best course for monetary policy, so for interest rates, in the first place? So the expectations—you know, we were expecting prior to the war a stable environment for interest rates, and a reduction maybe in the US, for example. This is no longer the case. Expectations have changed around interest rate policy, and then this is, of course, interest rates are a key, key variable in economic growth, and investment decisions, and so forth. Yeah, I mean to help people understand this a little bit, if we were in fact looking forward to stable rates or even rate cuts going into this conflict, I mean, that's, those are good times for an economy, right? I mean, that means things get revved up, there's more money in the system, more money flowing around, more ability to simply buy things and invest in things, and now it sounds like that is effectively off the table for the time being. In terms of alternatives, potentially for the Strait of Hormuz, are there any realistic alternatives out there? And, you know, what are we talking about potentially in terms of investment to actually create viable alternatives? Let's start with the geography of the area. If you look, if you open a map, you can see that alternatives are not there physically. So, what my colleagues in the region have done is to start shipping more and more goods to other ports. Jeddah is very important at this moment, Salalah in Oman, Khor Fakkan, and then build the so-called land bridges—so truck the distance from these ports to reach the rest of Saudi Arabia, Kuwait, Bahrain, UAE, Qatar, Iraq. This is of course a very difficult operation. You have to imagine when a big vessel arrives in a port, it can have 20,000 containers, and each container can go on one truck. So, you need a lot of trucks to replace the agility of normal port operations in the area. So, these alternatives don't exist physically, one-on-one, in terms of replacement. Now, if we are thinking more around oil, I'm not an expert, but my understanding is that it's very difficult to ramp up production elsewhere. I understand that, for example, the US will export more this year than expected, but we are talking about hundreds of thousands of barrels, not huge amounts that can, by far, not compensate for this. And then, probably something that is being discussed at this moment is also building more pipelines. But that is something else of course that doesn't happen within weeks, and it's especially difficult to do in the time of a conflict. And not cheap, and not cheap either. So, yes, it is very difficult to find quick solutions, and that's why, I think, the expectation is that in the short term, if the crisis continues, we will have to live with this gap between demand and supply of fossil fuels and hence, the fairly grim expectations among chief economists around what that will mean for growth and inflation. What you're saying essentially is that whatever shape this Strait of Hormuz comes into, whatever sort of the resolution is, whatever that looks like in the end, the global economy is kind of going to have to deal with that, as opposed to imagining that this whole new set of infrastructure is going to appear and then we can just not rely on the strait anymore. Difficult to imagine at this moment, and I guess that is why we're all following very closely the developments on an hourly basis since February 28. But, hey, I think there is something, there's something interesting happening with straits. All of a sudden, you know, there's a lot of discussion around these choke points. The Strait of Hormuz is not the only strait that is very important; there are several out there, and probably two that, if I look at a map, are calling my attention. One is Panama. I'm not the only one that is looking at Panama; definitely the US administration has become very sensitive lately about who operates the canal and the terminals there. So, this is per se interesting. But there is also an alarm raised by scientists about El Niño this year, which could create a risk of low water in the canal. And this happened already in recent times: in 2023, there was another season where the levels were low and so fewer ships could pass. So, it is possible to go around Panama—it has been done also in 2023, for example, when the water was low—but of course, you start to put a map in front of you and say, "Okay, this one, there we cannot pass; there we cannot pass. This is problematic." Fewer ships, so this is another strait that calls for attention. Yeah, no, that's a great point because people are obviously thinking of one particular risk now—conflict, war—but climate change presents its own set of challenges, it sounds like. Just to help people understand a little bit in terms of relative significance: Strait of Hormuz relative to the Panama Canal. I mean, if we did see a disruption in the Panama Canal, how would that sort of measure up to what we've been seeing recently in the Strait of Hormuz? It's not as disruptive, as I said, because there is possibility to go around it. So, it is less disruptive in a way, but, of course, every time you have to find new routes, new ways, there are capacity constraints at some point that can be hit. You know, if you start closing more than one strait, then when you sum it up, it can become problematic to deal with. We all, well, most of us anyway, probably remember the supply chain issues that emerged during COVID. Is this a case where we sort of forgot those lessons, or was real progress made after those big disruptions that have actually gotten us to a better place than we were before? So, I think that the world has not forgotten about it, but in the world we live in today, I think the view around trade and supply chains has changed. So, for a long time, they were mostly a tool for economic growth, and now they're rather a tool for foreign policy and security policy. And this is also the result of all the changes that have happened in the last decades with globalization, which has created niches of specialization in the global economy. So everyone does what they're best at and they purchase the rest, but this has also created winners and losers, especially within Western countries. And there is also the fact that the forces of globalization themselves have, in a way, planted the seed of the future change. There is a lot of investment that went into China, that went into Southeast Asia, and this has turned the region a bit into the factory of the world. Now, you know, fast forward to 2026, we are in a sort of transition phase for the global order, and disruptions at this phase are probably inevitable. Is there anything, beyond what we've sort of been discussing here relative to the Strait of Hormuz and choke points, is there anything else about the global economy in general that's kind of top of mind for you these days? Well, several things, I have to say. Of course, I'm following on an hourly basis the development of the conflict in the Middle East, but there is a lot that is happening, and we should not forget that this is not the only war at this moment in the world, unfortunately. There is climate change; the fact that we're not talking about it doesn't mean it's not going to happen, or that it's not happening already. So, El Niño could be another important source of struggles in some parts of the world. And we talked about the risk of low water in the Panama Canal, but it could also exacerbate the problems that are now being generated by the fact that there is less fertilizer being traded, and prolong, in a way, the shock for the agricultural sector in some countries. I'm thinking of India, for example. US tariffs... Can't forget tariffs, yes, of course. Yes, can't forget, I can't forget tariffs for sure. In July, the 10% tariff on all US imports will expire—the one that followed the ruling of the Supreme Court—and there will be new tariffs in place. But we still don't know much about on which sectors, on which countries, or how high they will be, so there's still a lot of uncertainty on this topic. And still on the US, there is the USMCA renegotiation looming, also during summer. And I think it's interesting there how Mexico and Canada have positioned themselves in a very different way. Mexico has done its best to align itself to the trade policy of the US, and Canada is in a different position, more in a clashing position with the US, so let's see what happens with that negotiation. Then, there is a lot happening in the field of trade policy, also outside the US. There is of course concern of protectionism spreading further against China because we have seen Chinese exports incredibly strong in the last three years, possibly crowding out exports from other places or even domestic production in some countries. So, that's definitely becoming a sensitive topic in many capitals. But at the same time, what I find super interesting is how trade agreements that were thought impossible, like EU-Mercosur or EU-India, have all of a sudden been signed. So, there is a lot happening in the field of trade policy in both directions. There is new dynamism, I think. Brussels, the EU, and India are a bit in the center of it, so let's see, once the dust settles, where the balance is between these two forces at play. And in general, I think if I take another step back and look at the outlook, there are these three forces at play in the economy right now. There is definitely, of course, the energy shock we discussed at length. There is also this AI investment boom that keeps fueling growth in the US and in a lot of countries in Asia, like Korea and Taiwan, for example. And then, there is a lot of fiscal support in the economy that is there as a floor. The fiscal deficit in the US last year was 6% of GDP—we're talking about 6% of a huge GDP, so a lot of support for the economy—and close to 3% in the Eurozone, also. So, I wonder where the balance will be between these three forces: the oil shock, the AI investment, and the fiscal support. Ilaria Maselli, thanks so much for joining us. Thank you so much for having me. Ilaria Maselli of Maersk, who was speaking to John Letzing. You can find the Chief Economists Outlook—search for that, or click the link in the show notes. Please follow Radio Davos wherever you get podcasts, and if you like the show, please give us a rating or review and tell your friends. This episode of Radio Davos was written and presented by me, Robin Pomeroy. Studio production was by Taz Kelleher. Radio Davos will be back next week, but for now, thanks to you for listening, and goodbye.

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent holds White House press briefing — 5/28/2026

CNBC Television(3.4M)·31:37·21.3K views·2026-05-28
Scott BessentSecretary of the Treasury
TL;DRTreasury Secretary Scott Bessent highlighted the nationwide rollout of the newly launched "Trump Accounts" for children, reported on a historic tax filing season with rising refunds, and addressed critical foreign policy negotiations regarding Iran and the Strait of Hormuz.

TL;DR

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent highlighted the nationwide rollout of the newly launched "Trump Accounts" for children, reported on a historic tax filing season with rising refunds, and addressed critical foreign policy negotiations regarding Iran and the Strait of Hormuz.

Economic Performance & the Launch of "Trump Accounts"

  • Trump Accounts Initiative: The official app has launched on major platforms ahead of the program's formal start on July 4th, with nearly 6 million children already registered via TrumpAccounts.gov.
    • Financial Seed Money: The Treasury will provide a $1,000 seed investment for children born during the Trump administration.
    • Financial Literacy: Designed to bring 38% of Americans who currently have no equity exposure into the financial system, the app includes "six learning pods" to educate families on investing.
  • Tax Filing Milestones: Bessent reported the "largest tax cuts in American history," with the average refund reaching nearly $3,300 (an 11% year-over-year increase).
    • Signature Tax Cuts: Approximately 62 million returns (44% of filers) claimed at least one signature tax cut, such as tax exemptions on tips, overtime, and Social Security benefits, or auto loan interest deductibility.
  • Macroeconomic Outlook: Real GDP has grown 2.6% over the past four quarters, with the Atlanta Fed’s GDPNow predicting 3.8% growth for the second quarter.
    • Labor & Inflation: Unemployment remains at 4.3%, and the latest month-over-month PCE inflation came in lower than expected at 0.2%.
    • Federal Reserve Relations: Bessent noted a "new day at the Fed" under Chair Walsh, stating they hold weekly meetings to balance growth and inflation.

Energy Dominance & Middle East Negotiations

  • Iran Ceasefire & Nuclear Negotiations: The administration is actively negotiating a potential 60-day ceasefire extension. Bessent emphasized that any final deal requires Iran to turn over highly enriched uranium, abandon its nuclear program, and guarantee free transit through the Strait of Hormuz.
    • Sanctions Leverage: Bessent credited "kinetic action" and "economic pressure" for forcing Iran to discuss its nuclear program, noting that any future sanctions relief would be slow and strictly contingent on concessions.
  • Energy Market Resilience: Bessent attributed falling energy prices (oil down 10% in May) to U.S. energy dominance and deregulation. The U.S. remains the world's largest energy exporter, which insulates the domestic economy from geopolitical disruptions in the Strait of Hormuz.
  • Diplomatic Relations with Oman: Following a public warning from President Trump, Bessent confirmed he spoke with the Omani ambassador, who guaranteed there are no plans to toll the Strait of Hormuz.
  • Global Sanctions Posture:
    • Russia: Bessent defended the administration's record, noting that President Trump authorized unprecedented sanctions against Russia's two largest oil companies, Lukoil and Rosneft.
    • Cuba: The Treasury is working closely with Secretary of State Marco Rubio, utilizing a "carrot and stick" approach to adjust sanctions based on the Cuban government's cooperation with humanitarian aid.

Domestic Investigations, Technology, & National Symbols

  • Proposed $250 Bill: Legislation has been introduced in the House and Senate to allow a living person, Donald J. Trump, to appear on a new $250 bill celebrating the nation's 250th anniversary. The Treasury has prepared technical designs in advance should Congress pass the law.
  • Antifa Funding Investigation: The Treasury is making "substantial progress" alongside the FBI to track down the funders of Antifa.
    • IRS Form 990 Guidance: The IRS is issuing new guidance requiring non-profit organizations to know their grant recipients, holding them accountable if they fund groups that engage in violence or civil rights suppression.
  • AI Security and Supremacy: The Treasury is working in partnership with major AI development labs regarding safety and national security risks associated with advanced large language models (such as those from Anthropic). Bessent stated the goal is to optimize the balance between innovation and safety to preserve the U.S.'s significant lead over China.

Notable Quotes

"Thirty-eight percent of Americans have no exposure to equities. They don't share in the great innovation, the machine that is the American economy... I think we're going to create a generation of shareholders." — *Scott Bessent, Treasury Secretary*
"This administration, President Trump, has done something that no other administration has been able to do. We have gotten the Iranians to talk about their nuclear program and to perhaps commit to not having one." — *Scott Bessent, Treasury Secretary*
"There's no cure for stupid." — *Scott Bessent, Treasury Secretary (responding to California Governor Gavin Newsom's proposed 100% state tax on federal anti-weaponization fund payouts)*
Good afternoon, everyone. Good. I'd like to say it's a pleasure to be here today. This is my warm-up for my Senate and House hearings next week, so I don't want anyone yelling, "Reclaim my time." So, let's start out with Trump Accounts. Trump Accounts, I believe, are the most important benefit for young people since the GI Bill. Today, the app is now available on all major platforms, bringing the President's vision directly to American homes. We had a historic tax filing season. We passed the largest tax cuts in American history. And to remind everyone, Democrats, every Democrat, voted against those programs. We've had sustained and resilient GDP growth. Real GDP has risen 2.6% over the past four quarters. Atlanta Fed's GDPNow predicts 3.8% for this quarter, the second quarter. Part of our resilience stems from our energy dominance. Natural gas is down, that's my belief, and the futures market is showing us oil will be lower than pre-conflict levels. The United States has never exported so much energy or produced so much energy. Main Street prosperity: the average 401k is up almost $30,000 since President Trump's inauguration. President Trump RX has already saved Americans over $600 million. I'm sure all of you will want to report on that. And the unemployment rate remains low at 4.3%. AI NOW Summit 2026 | Paris | Opening Keynote

Mistral AI(13.5K)·44:40·7.1K views·2026-05-28
Arthur MenschCo-founder & CEO, Mistral AI
TL;DRMistral AI is transitioning from generic AI to a fully verticalized, full-stack enterprise offering, combining owned compute infrastructure with tailored multimodal models and agentic workflows.
By integrating physical and language intelligence through strategic acquisitions...

TL;DR

Mistral AI is transitioning from generic AI to a fully verticalized, full-stack enterprise offering, combining owned compute infrastructure with tailored multimodal models and agentic workflows. By integrating physical and language intelligence through strategic acquisitions like Emmi AI and Koyeb, Mistral is enabling deep customization for global leaders in engineering, finance, and public services.

Full-Stack Infrastructure Control

  • The Case for Vertical Integration: Mistral operates under the conviction that deploying enterprise AI successfully requires owning the full stack, from physical chips and compute operations up to the business application layer.
  • Securing Capacity Amid Supply Crunches:
    • Bruyères-le-Châtel Data Center: A 40-megawatt training facility south of Paris built with Eclarian, optimized for maximum speed and hardware precision. It will be fully operational by the end of the summer.
    • Paris Inference Data Center: A newly announced 10-megawatt facility launching this summer near Bruyères-le-Châtel, dedicated entirely to customer inference capacity.
    • Borlänge Data Center (Sweden): A new facility slated for development through 2027 that will host NVIDIA's next-generation Vera Rubin architecture.
  • Serverless Integration: Fully integrated Koyeb, a serverless acquisition, to host Model Context Protocol (MCP) connections, sandboxes, and developer GPU access.

Next-Generation Multimodal & Agentic Models

  • The Value of Customization: Open-source weights allow enterprises to fine-tune models on proprietary data, outperforming generic, off-the-shelf closed models that lack organizational context.
  • Mistral Medium 3.5 Consolidation: Integrates several specialized legacy models into a single, natively multimodal intelligence.
    • Deprecated Models: Native multimodal reasoning now replaces the standalone Pixtral (images), Magistral (reasoning/math), and Devstral (coding) models.
    • Agentic Search: Equipped with tool-calling capabilities to search across complex enterprise environments like Google Drive, Slack, and Notion while significantly reducing hallucinations.
  • Specialized Small Models: The rise of agentic, token-hungry workflows has renewed focus on smaller, highly efficient models.
    • Mistral OCR: A 1-billion parameter model that converts document pages into structured text and images, processing thousands of pages per minute on a single GPU.
    • Voxtral: Moving toward a real-time "duplex" speech-to-speech model supporting voice cloning, speaker diarization, and live translation across 200+ languages.
  • Future Roadmap: Mistral Large 4 is scheduled for release by summer 2025, featuring training tailored for cyber defense, finance, and advanced engineering physics.

Enterprise Solutions & Developer Tools

  • The Vibe Product Family: Transitioning the conversational assistant Le Chat into the Vibe ecosystem to bridge the gap between software engineering and knowledge work.
    • Vibe for Work: A web application and task planner designed to automate everyday business tasks, summarize communications, and manage API connections.
    • Vibe for Code: Web-based session monitoring, a CLI, and a new VS Code extension allowing developers to coordinate parallel debugging sessions on any device.
  • Enterprise Governance Tools:
    • Studio: An on-prem or cloud-deployable management hub that lets enterprises govern model connections, artifacts, and secure data boundaries.
    • Forge: A localized training tool deployed where client data lives to adapt Mistral’s base models to highly specific domain languages, esoteric code bases, and unique modalities.

Strategic Verticals & High-Impact Partnerships

  • Industrial Engineering & Physical AI: By acquiring Emmi AI, Mistral is combining language models with physical intelligence to build simulations ranging from microscale fluid dynamics to molecular dynamics.
    • Airbus: Partnering across commercial, helicopter, and defense divisions to apply AI to design, engineering data, and aircraft operations.
    • BMW: Collaborating on engineering and car crash simulations to accelerate R&D cycles.
    • ASML: Developed custom AI code review agents and troubleshooting diagnostics, speeding up operations 120 times over previous methods with equivalent accuracy.
  • Global Finance Integration:
    • BNP Paribas: Deployed local, energy-efficient models on-premises, reducing incomplete KYC retail banking files from 80% to 10% and scaling the CIB platform to 65,000 corporate users.
    • HSBC & Abanca: Leveraging on-prem deployments to build proprietary trading alpha and improve decision-making on trading floors.
  • Sovereign Government & Global Tech:
    • Public Sector Initiatives: Collaborating with France (France Travail), Luxembourg, Singapore (HTX), Greece, and Morocco to deploy AI administrative assistance while honoring regional laws and cultural nuances (e.g., Darija and Amazigh languages).
    • Amazon: Partnering with Amazon to power high-quality non-English interactions for Alexa Plus.

Notable Quotes

"We are in the business of transforming electrons into tokens and intelligence." — Arthur on the physical footprint of AI

It makes a very big difference compared to an off-the-shelf closed model that is going to be the same model used by millions of people." — Guillaume on model customization
"The core issue that we are trying to solve is to catch the issues as early on as possible... Together with Mistral, we built a custom solution for AI agents that meet our security and data needs. These AI agents act as an always-on code reviewer so that they can flag risky code changes." — ASML spokesperson on engineering quality control
The AI running your business wasn't made for you. It was made for everyone. You get a tool. They get control. You get the same generic AI as your competitors. No way to translate your expertise into an edge. No way to solve your unique business problems. That's not power. That's a limitation. Your AI transformation starts with moving from artificial intelligence to actual intelligence. Based on your business, your knowledge, and your work. This is how you get AI that diagnoses an issue on the factory floor in real-time. That helps banks deploy tailored solutions for risk and compliance. It's about custom intelligence, whether it's running on a physical edge device for vehicles, or embedded in complex operations of robotics. Innovators aren't interested in AI that was made for everyone. They're working to solve the world's hardest problems, to lead their industry, and to bring ideas to reality. That's why it's time to move from generic to tailored AI. Time to take control. Time to own your AI transformation.
