More and more, the compute world has been getting supply-constrained, and so one of the reasons we've been doing all of this and developing all of these data center capabilities is to secure compute capacity not only for ourselves but also for our customers. And so, through this data center, we'll be able to offer a fully verticalized offering of Mistral products that we control from the application layer down to the hardware. And with this, I'll leave it to Guillaume to tell you the latest on our models. Yes, thank you Timothée. Hello everyone. I'm Guillaume, thank you for being here today. So, I'm going to talk a little bit about our models, what we have built so far, and also what's coming next. Every time we train models, we have a lot of things we care a lot about. One of them, for instance, is open source, as Arthur mentioned before. We have been releasing the weights of pretty much all of our models so far. It was something we have been doing since day one, since the release of our first model, Mistral 7B. It's also something we were doing even before Mistral. This mission is really important to us. It's one of the missions of the company to give AI to everyone, put AI in the hands of everyone, so that everybody can benefit from AI and how powerful it is. And one nice thing you can do if you release the weights of your model, if you open-source your model, is that you can customize them. So, you can take your model, and you can train it on all your company data. Sometimes if you have a company that is several years old or potentially that has been there for decades, you have a tremendous amount of information that you have been collecting through the years. And what people don't realize before they do it is how much better a model can be if you fine-tune it—if you basically continue the pre-training of this model on the data of your company. It makes a very big difference compared to an off-the-shelf closed model that is going to be the same model used by millions of people. The issue if you use a closed model is that every time you start a new conversation with it, every time you start a new workflow, this model will know nothing about your company. So, you will have to give it all the context every single time. So, yes, it's going to work, these models are smart, but it's going to be very inefficient. Well, if you train your model on all your company's data, in a sense, the model will be trained on potentially millions of documents, and it's going to know your company better than, I mean, literally anyone in the company. These models can encompass and integrate a huge amount of information. So, they will know the company very well and be very efficient. So, that's really something that we encourage people to do, that we do with many of our customers, because there are massive gains when you actually do this customization and fine-tuning. Something related to this is also having small and efficient models. So, since the very beginning, we have worked on compressing as much as possible the intelligence into our models—trying to get a model as small as possible but as good as possible. When we released Mistral 7B, it was the best open-source model of this size, better than much larger models. When we released Mixtral 8x7B, it was like two and a half years ago, it was the best open-source model, much better than much larger models as well. So, making small and efficient models has always been a focus for us. And it was not that important a couple of years ago. If you remember, before, people were saying that models don't need to be this fast because people were only using these models for chatbots or basically this multi-turn instruction-following conversation. But today, the situation is very different. Today, we are building these agentic workflows. These models are running in the background, they are doing a lot of actions, a lot of tool calls, so they are extremely token-hungry, much more than before. So, what we are seeing today is actually a comeback of these small models and efficient models. It was not something people were interested in before, but now it's very different because of these new workflows that we are seeing today and these agentic capabilities. Another thing that we really care a lot about is the multilingual capabilities. So, usually, what we have seen is that people train models on the languages they speak, the languages in the country they come from. For instance, the US models will be very English-centric, and the ones in China are trained on English and Chinese. For us, we really try to train our models on as many languages as possible since the very beginning. The first Mistral models were trained on dozens of languages. The next ones that we are going to release are trained on more than 200 languages, basically every language our classifier can identify. And this is really important; it makes a big difference for many of our customers. And it's also cheaper if you actually take this into account from the very beginning. Because if you are dealing with a new language, a language that the model has not been trained on, not only is it not going to be as good, but also it's going to be very inefficient. Your tokenizer is not going to be used to this language, so you'll need a lot of tokens to process a very small amount of text. So, yes, we have always put a lot of energy into basically making sure our models are really good in all European languages, Asian languages, etc. The first models we released were text-only models, so they were fairly simple. Models like a couple of years ago were much simpler compared to what we have today. There were no agentic workflows, and there was no multimodal. The first modality we added was basically giving the model the ability to process images, which is very important when you want to process research papers, academic documents, or industrial papers with a lot of figures, complex diagrams, etc. Or even if you want to have your model being able to process slides and read them. Sometimes you cannot linearize the information into text, so you really want the model to be able to read this information. So, now, all our models are natively multimodal. An example of a model that is multimodal and very small and efficient is Mistral OCR. So, it's a model that is very simple, it does only one thing: it takes some basically document and will convert it into a list of text and images that you can feed into your large models. When we actually do this fine-tuning of models on top of our customers' documents, when they have millions of PDF files or Word documents they want to train the model on, the first thing we have to do is convert all of this into a format the model can read. And this is where we use Mistral OCR. It's a very small model with 1 billion parameters. It can process thousands of pages per minute on a single GPU. And here, if you work with some competitors, what they will recommend if you want to do this is to actually use a large model, ask it "can you transcribe this document?", and it's going to work—it's not a difficult task—but it's going to be extremely inefficient. You will be using a model that is composed of hundreds of billions of parameters, while here it's a 1B model, so extremely small, extremely fast, and it makes a big difference. Another example of a modality we added, on a small model as well, is Voxtral. So, it was our first audio model. We released the first version last year. Initially, it was just an ASR model, so that can do automatic speech recognition. It was basically transcribing, so it was a 24B model, really good, and a few months after this we released a model that is a 3B model, so 10 times smaller, but also can actually process more languages. We also added a lot of features important for many of our customers, such as language identification, speaker diarization, so you can know who is saying what at which moment. And yes, we also added some real-time capability, so now you don't have to wait for the end of a speech to be able to process it. You can process it on the fly, and the transcription will appear as you speak, which is also useful if you want to do some live translation, for instance. And a few months ago we released our last speech model, Voxtral TTS, which is a model that can generate speech. So, it takes text as input, generates the voice, and it can take the voice that you want—you can even clone your voice. It's really efficient, and now that we have done all of these different speech-in, speech-out, and real-time models, now we are combining all of these capabilities into a single model that we are going to release in a couple of months. It's going to be our final Voxtral that will be a duplex model—a model you can talk to, and it will talk to you back in real-time. Yes. Here is another example of a model, Mistral Medium 3.5, that is a combination of many different models and capabilities. I mentioned before we had some multimodal model, it was called Pixtral. Pixtral no longer exists. It was a model that was really good at processing images, but now all our models are natively multimodal, so we don't have a separate model for images; it's going to be part of all of our models. There is another model that we released one year ago called Magistral. It was a model really good at doing reasoning, so processing some complex math questions or physics questions, etc. Now we no longer have Magistral; this model is deprecated because all our models will natively be doing reasoning. We also had some Devstral model that was a model for developers, really good at coding, writing code, writing PRs, finding issues, etc. So here, the same: we also integrated these coding capabilities into Mistral Medium 3.5, so we no longer have a Devstral model. And the latest thing that we integrated is the agentic search capability, which is basically giving the model the ability to do some search on some very complex environments. For instance, if you deploy a Mistral Medium into an enterprise, and you ask it some question like "can you summarize this topic for me?" or "tell me information about this or that", what the model can do is use some API calls to search the information on Google Drive, on Slack, on Notion, wherever it might be, even potentially in your emails. This is also why it's good if it's on-premise or on your own server, so you don't have to worry about confidentiality and privacy. And this model will be really good at identifying if the information is there, and if not, searching further, sometimes searching for maybe like 10 minutes—doing like dozens and dozens of tool calls. And what we found is that by training the model to do this, we also reduced a lot of the hallucinations, because the model is trained to detect if it found the solution or not. And if it didn't find the solution, it will tell you that it doesn't have it, basically. So, it's also reducing a lot of the hallucinations, which is very important if you want to deploy this model into some vertical and some industries. So, now we are continuing to add capabilities to all our Mistral models. We are working now on the next large version of our basically Mistral Large 4, which will potentially, a priori, be there in a couple of months at most, during the summer. We are adding many capabilities, for instance, in industrial applications, such as fluid dynamics, computational chemistry, computer-aided design, but also like some cyber defense, cyber attack kind of capabilities, and even finance, yes. Neuralink's DJ Seo: Inside the Race to Connect Brains and AI

Sequoia Capital(208.0K)·24:59·7.2K views·2026-05-28
DJ SeoCo-founder and President, Neuralink Shaun MaguirePartner, Sequoia Capital Elon MuskFounder, Neuralink
TL;DRNeuralink is transitioning from early clinical trials to a scalable platform, leveraging deep vertical integration and surgical robotics to restore human motor and visual faculties.
Ultimately, the company aims to bridge the input/output bottleneck between humans and AI,...

TL;DR

Neuralink is transitioning from early clinical trials to a scalable platform, leveraging deep vertical integration and surgical robotics to restore human motor and visual faculties. Ultimately, the company aims to bridge the input/output bottleneck between humans and AI, transforming artificial intelligence into a high-bandwidth "exocortex" that integrates directly with human thought.

Current Clinical Progress and Product Pipeline

  • Telepathy (Motor Prosthesis): Neuralink’s first commercial product allows quadriplegic and ALS patients to control computers and digital interfaces purely through neural intent.
      • Convoy Robotics: A specialized team within Neuralink is translating these motor signals to control physical, assistive robotic devices, restoring physical independence in the real world.
      • Patient Milestones: Neuralink has successfully implanted over 20 human patients, dropping the "digital barrier" to let users control devices, play games, and communicate.
  • Blindsight (Vision Restoration): Neuralink’s next major product targets total vision loss, bypassing damaged eyes or optic nerves.
      • Mechanism of Action: An external camera captures environmental data and writes it directly to the visual cortex via electrical stimulation, creating artificial visual perceptions called "phosphenes."
      • Timeline: The next-generation human-grade device is currently in pre-clinical testing, with human trials targeted by the end of 2026.
  • The Human Element: DJ Seo emphasized that the success of these trials relies heavily on the resilience of patient families and caregivers (such as Nolan’s mother Mia, Brad's wife Tiffany, and Ken's wife Cheryl).

Scaling, Surgical Robotics, and Vertical Integration

  • Designing for Scale: Unlike traditional academic BCI projects (like the rigid, through-the-skin Utah arrays of the 2000s), Neuralink was designed from day one to scale to millions of users.
  • Surgical Automation: To make implantation scalable and safe, Neuralink built proprietary surgical robots. The ultimate goal is to make BCI implantation as fast, automated, and accessible as LASIK eye surgery.
  • Vertical Integration: Adopting the manufacturing and iteration frameworks of other Elon Musk-led ventures, Neuralink manages its entire pipeline in-house—from semiconductor chip fabrication and robotic engineering to clinical deployment. This minimizes external bottlenecks and accelerates iteration loops.
  • Regulatory Pathways: Neuralink plans to leverage its first FDA clinical safety approvals to establish a pipeline for rapid regulatory updates, utilizing regulatory mechanisms like PMA supplements to easily expand and upgrade device capabilities.

The Intersection of BCIs and Artificial Intelligence

  • The AI Bottleneck: The core premise of Neuralink's founding in 2016 was solving the extreme input/output (I/O) bottleneck between human communication speeds (typing, speaking) and exponential AI compute capabilities.
  • AI as an "Exocortex": In the long term, AI will act as a tertiary cognitive layer above the human neocortex.
      • Direct Intent Transfer: The ultimate ceiling of BCI technology is bypassing legacy translation systems (like mouse, keyboard, and spoken language) to enable direct, uncompressed, high-fidelity, and multimodal conceptual transfers between human minds and machine networks.
  • Neural Foundational Models: Neuralink has begun utilizing transformer architectures to build "neural foundational models." By training and fine-tuning these large-scale models on neural datasets from their 20+ active patients, they are uncovering counterintuitive, highly complex patterns of brain activity.

Engineering Culture and Talent Acquisition

  • The "All Green Light" Schedule: Seo highlighted Elon Musk’s aggressive timelines as a rigorous first-principles engineering tool rather than arbitrary pressure.
      • Principle: Teams must map out timelines assuming zero administrative burdens, zero shipping delays, and zero man-made bottlenecks. This forces engineers to design around physical limits rather than institutional norms, stripping away 80% to 90% of perceived constraints.
  • Recruiting Focus: Neuralink does not require a background in neuroscience. The company prioritizes "hardcore engineers" who can solve physical, real-world problems.
      • Critical Open Roles: Neuralink is aggressively hiring for embedded software and firmware engineers to handle high-bandwidth data, alongside talent to solve complex data labeling challenges for machine learning.

Notable Quotes

"I do think in some time horizon AI basically becomes an exocortex similar to our neocortex... and really that's what kind of drives a lot of the cognitive abilities that we have. It's ultimately all about the bandwidth." — DJ Seo
"What I've learned is that [Elon Musk's] insanely aggressive schedule is not really some arbitrary management tactics, but it's really used as a rigorous engineering and first-principle tool." — DJ Seo
"The real breakthrough is going to happen when you can bypass all of [the legacy inputs] and be able to compute on the raw intent itself... things just seem impossible without scale, but things just become inevitable with scale." — DJ Seo

And the reason I believe that's true is, you know, similar to how your present self is very different than your past and future selves, sometimes, maybe not unlike how others are different. And, you know, if your goal and fulfillment comes from striving to do the best for yourself, I—you know, I do think that you can apply the same principle to say: if you strive to help others, there's a lot of fulfillment that comes in. And because I believe that, this is something that, you know, I personally, and as well as many others at Neuralink, find extreme fulfillment in—being able to help those that really cannot help themselves. And I—I think it's—it's an extremely inspiring and motivating thing to kind of stand at the—the Team Human side and be able to ignite that sort of fire of hope, which—which is something that we hear from our current participants and future patients every day. So, in the video we watched in the beginning, it talked about this next product, Blindsight. What can you—you know, what can you tell us about Blindsight, you know, what's coming? Yeah, I guess first of all, Blindsight is a completely different paradigm shift than our motor prosthesis. So, this would be for patients that are blind or have total loss of vision from diseases of the eyes or optic nerve, where you would have an external camera that captures the scene and then be able to directly write in the back of your head, in the visual cortex, which, you know, through electrical stimulation that changes the polarization environment of the—the neurons, can create these things called phosphenes. And the more electrodes you have, the more pixels you have, essentially, and you'll be able to regain vision. And this is something that we're very excited about, and, you know, where—where it is right now is that we have currently the next-gen version that we want to go to humans with in pre-clinical testing. So hopefully, you know—I might eat my words—but hopefully by... And you do not need to make any predictions that you're uncomfortable making, so... End of this year. So everyone in this room's thinking about AI—it's a stacked audience—how do you think things play out at the interface of BCIs and—and AI? With the simplest thing being just, you know, thinking straight to Grok prompts coming, you know, sure Nolan has this. Yeah, be careful what you wish for, I guess. Yeah, I—I do think in some time horizon, AI basically becomes an exocortex, similar to our neocortex, which is basically a layer above our limbic system, and really that's what kind of drives a lot of the cognitive abilities that we—we have. And, you know, it's ultimately all about the bandwidth, it's really all about that interface. And, you know, I think if you really think about the ultimate ceiling of this technology, I—I think it's really enabling direct, uncompressed, high-fidelity, and multimodal transfer of concepts. The "I learned kung fu" in *The Matrix*—that's—that's one, but even maybe beyond that, right? Is, you know, your—you know, where this—this is obviously still far out in the future, I—you know, our—our current system still is converting neural intents into legacy systems like keyboard and mouse control and language, but, you know, I think the real breakthrough is going to happen when—when you can bypass all of that and be able to, you know, sort of compute on the raw intent itself. And I—I think it's actually not super distant in the future. In many ways, I think we're seeing this with modern AI systems, where, you know, these transformer architectures are doing incredible things. There's nothing fundamentally that prevents them from, say, training on and learning about the actual latent manifolds of the neural systems. And, you know, I think the key component here really is scale. I—I think, again, we're seeing this with the AI revolution, where things just seem impossible without scale, but things just become inevitable with scale. And I think similar things are going to happen, and, you know, where even at our scale of 20 or so participants, we're starting to see some interesting results in building, you know, what's called a neural foundational model. Where you have these state-of-the-art LLM models or transformer networks that are being fine-tuned with neural data, and there's a lot of really interesting patterns and things that it's learning that are very counterintuitive. So I think, you know, being able to sort of think about, and again, I think this is where scale is key, and that's where, in the world of atoms, it's—it's very unforgiving, and there's a lot of work to be done on the—on the ground. What is something you've learned from working with Elon, and bonus points if it's something underappreciated by the public? Yeah, I think I certainly also had a different conception of what "Elon time" is—the insanely aggressive schedule. What I've learned is that it's not really some arbitrary management tactic, but it's really used as a rigorous engineering and first-principles tool. You know, again, this is more applicable in the world of atoms, but we have this concept called the "all green light schedule," which is, you know, kind of forcing us to think about, if every single light is green—you know, there are no administrative burdens or delays, or no shipping delays or risk, or, you know, no legacy bottlenecks—how fast can you build it? And when you think from that point of view and vantage point, and literally strip—strip away any man-made bottlenecks, and you ask a question of how fast can you actually manufacture a chip, how fast can you design a robot and test it, it turns out, like, I don't know—I don't know what the right number is, but at least 80 to 90% of it is things that you think are the way they are just because you don't really think about it from that perspective. So, I think having that ability to, you know, frame everything from what is physically possible—a first-principles baseline for building something like that—I think is a really, really useful, just, framework for thinking. I—I should warn people that this is not for everyone, and it—it obviously comes at a cost in terms of, you know, shifting priorities and insane stress sometimes, and, you know, the cultural ramifications of having this sort of system. But when it works, boy, it's—it's something that is very empowering, and I think it's—it's also, yeah, just like the best thing ever to just see it unfold. When it works to get reusable rockets and electric vehicles. I'm going to ask one more question, then open it up to the audience. And this is a layup. You know, part of how I convinced DJ to come—because this guy's working very hard—was, you know, that maybe we can help him with recruiting. Like, there's all these AI foundation model representatives here where everyone's competing against each other, it's hard to do recruiting. This is something different; they—you know, they're—you know, they're on an island, scaling brain-computer interfaces. If any—the question for DJ is: are there any particular roles that would be very helpful to fill? And for any of you, if you have, you know, brilliant neuroscience friends or, you know, people doing BCIs at Stanford or Berkeley or anywhere, send them—like, send them to me, and I'll send them to—I'll screen them and then send them to DJ. But any roles that are particularly burning? Yeah, there's a saying—I'll—I'll answer that question, but I'll start by saying there's a saying at Neuralink that you don't have to be a brain surgeon to work at Neuralink. We really don't require any background in neuroscience. You know, most people, and especially like some of the best people at the company, are just hardcore engineers. So I'll—I'll state that, you know, we're just in general looking for just hardcore engineers, and, you know, you can learn the neuroscience, and, I mean, we'll—we'll learn as we go. And there's, again, like, a lot of our problems that we're solving are real, hardcore engineering challenges in the manufacturing, on the robotics, and things like that. I would say embedded software and firmware right now is—is a critical role for us to fill. And, you know, as I mentioned, I think we're starting to enter into an era where the scale of the data that we're getting, the quality of the data that we're getting, is extremely interesting to train some of the most state-of-the-art models. And there's also some inherent challenges associated with how do you even label this dataset—like, how do you actually know what the human's true intent is? So, how do you—how do you design it in a way such that, you know, you can—you can sort of clean up the data, which is obviously a big part of AI? So, any really smart person that doesn't want to work at a frontier lab. We have four minutes for questions. Eunice is bringing you a box. Question on how you choose when to, like, when to go deep on a product versus expanding to others. Like, what you built with Telepathy seems like an already transformational product, and yet, you know, you're already kind of onto the next thing. Like, how do you decide what to—like, when to expand into another area, and what are the biggest blockers right now to taking even what you've already built and making that more widely available? Yeah, that's a great question. You know, our whole strategy is beachhead and then expand, and then we try to make everything generalizable enough so that you don't just build a very specialized system for the motor cortex, and, you know, visual cortex, auditory cortex, etc. I mean, for us, there is a pretty huge bottleneck that is difficult to overcome, which is biology. And things that are somewhat easier to overcome but still difficult are regulatory and payment and that market dynamics. So, a lot of our strategy sort of hinges on what is the optimal time at which we should put some amount of resources so we can pipeline the crap out of things. So that once one of them is approved—which is going to be the first and the hardest one to get approval for, to build that clinical evidence of safety—you can then point to it, go through a much faster process, that's PMA supplements, 510(k)s, and things like that. But in order to do that, you—you want to build up some amount of pre-clinical and clinical data, so that's kind of how we think about the timing of launching different products. Dan. So, the video was—was incredibly moving, and I guess a lot of what you've talked about—maybe these are not exactly the right words—but are like therapies for patients that have diseases, and—and that's incredible. And then Shaun mentioned something about "I know kung—kung-fu." I'm wondering, like, is there a near—or, I'm curious what the near and maybe even longer-term view of, like, more things that are optional and for augmentation as opposed to, like, fixing problems is? Yeah, I think—yeah, so certainly our focus right now is restoration of lost function, and this is obviously a very serious medical device and serious surgery that you would have to go through. The—the way I think about all of this is that, at the end of the day, it's all about benefit-risk, and that's how everything's framed. As obviously, for people that are quadriplegic, their risk appetite for potential benefit is much, much higher. I do think that it's still not entirely clear what that transition to a non-medical use case would be, you know? At what point do you cross that benefit-risk threshold for someone who's otherwise healthy? Again, I think this—we have some ideas as to what—what that benefit side would be, and then obviously figuring out a way to reduce the risk as well. But one thing I will say is that there's a wonderful thing called off-label use once everything is approved, where if you can find the neurosurgeon, if you can find a way to pay for it yourself, you could get it for someone who's otherwise, like, healthy. We have 30 seconds, so rapid-fire question, then rapid-fire answer, and we're done. Do you think the hard problem of consciousness is a hard problem, and if you do, is there a pathway to solving it? Yeah, the hard problem of consciousness is an extremely hard problem. I do think that if you are able to inject new senses, there may be ways to quantify—quantitatively understand that. That was very fast. DJ, one of the most inspiring companies in the world, thank you for being here. Really inspiring.

Secretary of the Treasury Scott Bessent Briefs Members of the Media, May 28, 2026

The White House(3.1M)·31:49·51.2K views·2026-05-28
Scott BessentSecretary of the Treasury
TL;DRTreasury Secretary Scott Bessent highlighted the rollout of "Trump Accounts" designed to foster nationwide financial literacy and detailed the administration's economic achievements, while reiterating strict, non-negotiable red lines for ongoing security and energy negotiations with Iran.

TL;DR

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent highlighted the rollout of "Trump Accounts" designed to foster nationwide financial literacy and detailed the administration's economic achievements, while reiterating strict, non-negotiable red lines for ongoing security and energy negotiations with Iran.

"Trump Accounts" and Financial Literacy Initiative

  • Program Launch: The "Trump Accounts" app is now available on all major platforms, with the official program launching on July 4th. Nearly 6 million American children have already been signed up at trumpaccounts.gov.
  • Seed Capital: The Treasury Department will deposit a $1,000 seed investment for every child born during the Trump administration to encourage long-term investing.
  • Expanding Market Access: Noting that 38% of Americans currently have no exposure to equities, the initiative aims to build a "generation of shareholders" through six integrated online "learning pods" focused on real-time financial literacy.

Economic Indicators and Tax Policy Achievements

  • Historic Tax Refunds: Bessent reported a historic tax filing season following the passage of major tax cuts. The average refund rose to nearly $3,300, representing an 11% increase from last year.
  • Signature Tax Relief: Approximately 62 million returns (44% of filers) claimed at least one signature tax cut, including exemptions for tips, overtime, and reduced taxes on Social Security benefits (leaving 85% of seniors paying no tax on these benefits), as well as deductibility on American car loans.
  • Growth and Resilience: Real GDP has risen 2.6% over the past four quarters, with the Atlanta Fed’s GDPNow forecasting 3.8% growth for the second quarter.
  • Household Well-Being: The average 401(k) balance is up nearly $30,000 since the inauguration, and the unemployment rate remains low at 4.3%. Bessent stated that consumer spending remains robust across all income quintiles.

Foreign Policy, Sanctions, and Energy Dominance

  • Iran Negotiations: Bessent emphasized that President Trump will not agree to a "bad deal." Any potential agreement requires Iran to turn over highly enriched uranium, permanently forfeit its nuclear program, and guarantee free, open navigation through the Strait of Hormuz.
  • Aviation Sanctions: The U.S. is moving to restrict state-owned Iranian airlines. While allowing travel for valid humanitarian reasons and religious pilgrimages to Mecca and Medina, the U.S. will sanction any foreign entities that refuel these airlines, sell their tickets, or collect landing fees.
  • Strait of Hormuz Security: Following concerns over a potential conflict involving Oman, Bessent confirmed he spoke directly with the Omani ambassador, who assured him there are no plans to toll the Strait of Hormuz.
  • Russia Sanctions: Bessent defended the administration's record on national security, highlighting that the current Treasury department issued unprecedented sanctions against Russia's two largest oil companies, Lukoil and Rosneft.
  • Energy Independence: As the world's largest energy exporter, the U.S. remains insulated from global energy price fluctuations. Domestic production is at historic highs, and oil prices decreased by approximately 10% in May.

Treasury Operations, Currency, and Domestic Investigations

  • Proposed $250 Bill: Legislation is currently moving through Congress to wave the rule preventing living individuals from appearing on currency, which would allow President Donald J. Trump to be featured on a commemorative $250 bill. Bessent confirmed the Treasury is proactively preparing for this rollout should the legislation pass.
  • Anti-CBDC and Stablecoin Policy: The administration has completely ruled out the creation of a Central Bank Digital Currency (CBDC) to protect consumer privacy. Bessent urged Congress to pass the bipartisan Clarity Act to bring stablecoin and digital asset regulations onshore.
  • Non-Profit Regulations & Antifa: The Treasury and the FBI are making progress on an ongoing investigation into the funding of Antifa. To assist, the IRS is issuing new guidance on Form 990 to demand that non-profits verify their grant recipients are not engaging in violence.
  • IRS Settlement: Bessent declined to comment extensively on the settlement rolled over to the Department of Justice's weaponization fund, citing ongoing litigation. He referred further inquiries to Acting Attorney General Todd Blanche.

Notable Quotes

"I think this is going to be one of the greatest real-time financial literacy educations in history... American families are going to be able to look on their phone every day and see, and you know, I think it's going to drive people to try to understand what they've got here. I think we're going to create a generation of shareholders." — Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent
"This administration has been very clear: there will be no central bank digital currency, which I think is... would be the first step toward tracking, so we have taken that off the table." — Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent
"This administration, President Trump has done something that no other administration has been able to do: we have gotten the Iranians to talk about their nuclear program and to perhaps commit to not having one... our economic pressure has worked to bring them to the table." — Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent

Lisa Su Address to MIT Class of 2026

Massachusetts Institute of Technology (MIT)(969.0K)·14:59·163.6K views·2026-05-28
Lisa SuCEO of AMD
TL;DRIn her 2026 MIT Commencement Address, AMD CEO Lisa Su urges the graduating class to leverage their "engineer's instinct" to tackle the world's most complex challenges, emphasizing that true leadership in the era of AI requires human purpose, judgment, and the courage to "run toward the hardest problems.

TL;DR

In her 2026 MIT Commencement Address, AMD CEO Lisa Su urges the graduating class to leverage their "engineer's instinct" to tackle the world's most complex challenges, emphasizing that true leadership in the era of AI requires human purpose, judgment, and the courage to "run toward the hardest problems."

The MIT Foundation and the "Engineer's Instinct"

  • The Academic Crucible: Arriving at MIT in 1986 at age 17, Su experienced firsthand the rigorous environment of Next House and foundational courses like 6001 and 6002, which taught her the value of collaborative problem-solving through challenging "all-nighters."
  • Discovering Semiconductors:
      • Undergraduate Research (UROP): Working in Professor Hank Smith’s lab in Building 39, Su wore her first cleanroom suit to fabricate x-ray lithography mask blanks on delicate 2-inch wafers, sparking her lifelong passion for semiconductor technology.
      • PhD Mentorship: Under the guidance of her PhD advisor, Professor Dimitri Antoniadis, she learned to navigate failed experiments and build self-reliance.
  • Mens et Manus (Mind and Hand): Su highlighted how the MIT motto teaches students to think deeply, build practically, and persist through multiple failed experiments to solve seemingly impossible problems.
  • The Engineer's Instinct: Defined by Su as the methodology to face an apparently unsolvable problem, break it down, and work through it step-by-step. This instinct becomes exponentially more powerful when shared across a collaborative team.

Business Leadership: Running Toward Hard Problems

  • Early Career at IBM: Joining IBM at age 25, Su learned early on that engineering value is defined by the quality of one's ideas rather than age or tenure.
  • Career-Defining Advice: A mentor advised her to "run towards the hardest problems"—a strategy she credits as the foundation of her professional growth because hard problems reveal true capability.
  • The AMD Turnaround:
      • Calculated Risk: When Su became CEO of AMD 12 years prior, mentors warned her the job was too risky due to the company's financial struggles.
      • Strategic Pivot: Under her leadership, the team made a long-term bet on high-performance computing, eventually designing technology that powers the world's most sophisticated supercomputers.

The AI Wave and the Human Imperative

  • A Distinct Technological Shift: Unlike previous major shifts—the internet, mobile computing, and cloud computing—Su views Artificial Intelligence (AI) not merely as a productivity tool, but as an accelerator of scientific discovery across all disciplines.
  • Revolutionizing Healthcare: Su expressed immense optimism for AI's potential in medicine, noting it can empower clinical teams to aggregate global medical knowledge to deliver optimized, personalized patient care.
  • The Limit of Technology: Su asserted that technology alone cannot shape the future.
      • Human Responsibility: AI cannot determine which problems are worth solving, make ethical judgments in the absence of complete data, or take responsibility for real-world outcomes.
      • The Call for Leaders: The modern world requires multidisciplinary leaders who possess the purpose and courage to decide *how* and *why* these powerful computational tools should be deployed.

Defining and "Making" Your Own Luck

  • The True Nature of Luck: Su challenged the notion of passive luck, stating that the highly successful "make their own luck."
      • Risk-Taking: Luck is cultivated by actively choosing to work on incredibly difficult problems where the answers are not guaranteed.
      • Strategic Alignment: Success relies on surrounding oneself with peers who push individual boundaries and demand excellence.
  • A Charge to the Class of 2026: Su concluded by urging graduates to remain highly ambitious, rely on their rigorous training, and step confidently into professional environments where they may not initially know the answers.

Notable Quotes

"Engineering actually doesn't care how old you are. It actually cares whether you have good ideas." — Lisa T. Su
"Run towards the hardest problems. Hard problems really teach you what you're capable of." — Lisa T. Su
"Technology itself does not decide what the future looks like. The best people do." — Lisa T. Su
"Luck is not just being in the right place at the right time. It is taking the risk to work on something really hard... That's how you make your own luck." — Lisa T. Su
LISA T. SU: All right. Good afternoon, everyone. President Kornbluth, Chairman Gorenberg, trustees, faculty, family, friends, and most importantly, the MIT Class of 2026, congratulations. You've earned this. And I can tell you that standing here feels very different than I expected. I've given a lot of talks over the years, but this one is quite personal. And as you can imagine with Murphy's law, I somehow managed to lose my voice this week. So please bear with me if I sound a little rough. But I couldn't be happier to be here with you, and to give you a little bit of my story. I came to MIT in the fall of 1986. My parents dropped me off at Next House. I was 17 years old, born in Taiwan, raised in Queens, and I was pretty sure I was good at math. Then, of course, I walked into 6001 and 6002, and within about two weeks, I realized there were a lot of people at MIT who were very, very good at math. And I remember staring at those first problem sets thinking, "My goodness, these are super hard." And I'd never really pulled an all-nighter until freshman year. It was a new experience, but it was a lot of fun doing it together with your classmates. Now, MIT has this incredible way of pushing you further than you thought you could go. You wrestled with a problem. You blew up a circuit or two—yes, some of you may have—and then somehow the thing worked. And suddenly you realized that you could build something real. And that's when I started feeling like an engineer. One of the best parts of MIT is actually UROP, the opportunity as an undergraduate to work on real research, and that actually truly changed my life. My first UROP was in Professor Hank Smith's lab in Building 39, which Anantha tells me we are decommissioning and moving, making X-ray lithography mask blanks for a grad student. To be absolutely clear, at the time I had no idea what that actually meant. But I got to put on my first bunny suit, walk into a clean room, and start building devices on little 2-inch wafers, which at the time was pretty state of the art. And I learned very quickly to be careful, because those wafers were actually really delicate, and I definitely didn't want to be the one who broke them. But I ran a bunch of experiments. Most of them didn't work the way we expected, and so we adjusted and we tried again. And it was the coolest thing ever. For the first time, I wasn't just learning about technology in a classroom. I was part of a team trying to discover something new. And I remember thinking, "Wow, we can build things this small—things tiny enough to fit on a die the size of a coin, but powerful enough to change the world." And that's when I fell in love with semiconductors. Later, I had the privilege of working with Professor Dimitri Antoniadis, who became my PhD advisor. And that was where I really learned how to solve problems. I remember spending weeks in the clean room fabricating devices, and then bringing my wafers up to the test lab, only to discover they didn't behave the way I expected at all. And so I'd go back to Dimitri's office, and we'd figure out what we should do next. Looking back, that was probably where I grew the most at MIT. Because little by little, I went from a new grad student learning about the field to someone doing original research and actually contributing something new to the field. And along the way, I started believing in myself—not the confidence that I would always know the answer, but the confidence that even when I didn't know the answer, I could figure it out. What I realize now is MIT was teaching me something much bigger than semiconductor device physics: *Mens et Manus*—mind and hand. When I was a student, I thought it was just a motto. Now I think it captures exactly what makes MIT so special. MIT teaches you to think deeply, but it also teaches you to build, to test ideas, and to keep going when the first experiment, or even the fifth experiment, doesn't work. And over time, you start believing that you can solve problems that once felt impossible. I carried that feeling with me long after I left campus. When I joined IBM, I found myself starting all over again. IBM had hundreds of thousands of employees. I was 25 years old, wondering how I could possibly make a difference in a company that big. But I learned something important very quickly: engineering actually doesn't care how old you are. It actually cares whether you have good ideas. And one of my mentors told me something that I've never forgotten: run towards the hardest problems. At the time, I'm not sure I really knew what that meant, but I now realize this was the best advice I've ever received. Hard problems really teach you what you're capable of. So, fast forward a bit. Twelve years ago, I got a chance to put that lesson to the test. I had the opportunity to become CEO of AMD. AMD had a lot of potential, but the company had been through a few tough years. And some of my mentors thought taking that job was actually kind of risky. But for me, this was my dream job. This is what I'd been training for all those years: the opportunity to work at the bleeding edge of technology on problems that really mattered. And the first thing we had to do was figure out what we wanted to be when we grew up. This is a big company that had to figure this out. We made a long-term bet that high-performance computing would be the most important technology of the future, and we gave our talented team the room to think big. And over the next several years, we built technology to enable the most powerful computers in the world. And I can tell you, through all of it, I used every skill that MIT ever taught me and then some. I was trying to put it in words, and I decided that calling it the "engineer's instinct" was the right thing. It's the ability to face what seemed like an unsolvable problem, break it down, and methodically work through it step by step. But I also learned something else: the engineer's instinct is even more powerful when it becomes shared by a team. And the greatest satisfaction of my career has been bringing people together to do something more than any of us thought was possible. And that brings me to today and where you guys are. Over the last few decades, we've experienced several major technology shifts. The internet changed how we communicate. Mobile computing changed how we live. Cloud computing changed how we work. And now we're at the beginning of the AI wave. And to me, AI is really different from all those other waves. The way I think about it is it's not just a tool that can help us do things faster, because we have lots of tools. It's actually deeper than that. It has the potential to accelerate discovery in every field and help us solve problems that we've never been able to solve before. And to make it personal, the area that excites me the most is actually what we can do in medicine and health. I think we've all experienced firsthand what it feels like when someone you love is sick. And even with incredible doctors and the best care, you realize how hard it is for any one person, or any one team, to bring together all of the knowledge that has been gathered to help in that critical time of need. AI can help us change that. It can help doctors and researchers bring the world's best expertise to each patient and each loved one, and deliver the care that we want for the best chance of a successful outcome. And this, I think, is the promise of AI at its best. Now, the way to think about it makes each of us more capable. Whether you're talking about medicine, science, energy, or climate, I think you can say we may discover more in the next 10 years than we have in the last 30. But let me be clear about something: technology itself does not decide what the future looks like. The best people do. For everything that AI can do, AI can't decide which problems are worth solving. It can't make the hard judgments when the data is not there. It can't take responsibility for the outcomes. These are actually our responsibilities, and they matter now more than ever. This is why I feel this is such an extraordinary moment to graduate from MIT. Because the world does not just need people who know how to use powerful tools. It needs people who know what to use them for—people with a sense of purpose, judgment, and courage; people who look at a hard problem and say, "I know this is really, really important, and we can figure this out." And that is exactly who you have become here at MIT. So, here's what I want to leave you with. I've been very fortunate in many ways. I have great parents, I received an extraordinary education, and I've had the chance to work with great people. But I also believe I've been very lucky in my career. When people ask me for career advice, I often tell them, "Yes, you need to work really hard, but also understand that luck matters." And over time, I've come to believe that the best people find ways to make their luck. Luck is not just being in the right place at the right time. It is taking the risk to work on something really hard. It's challenging yourself. It's choosing problems where you may not know the answer. It's surrounding yourself with people who make you better. And yes, it's believing that you, the Class of 2026, can change the world. So be incredibly ambitious about what problems you choose to solve. Run toward the hardest ones, and trust what MIT has taught you: that engineer's instinct. That's how you make your own luck. I want to take a moment to acknowledge all the families and loved ones who are here in the audience today. None of these graduates got here without you. Thank you for believing in them, supporting them, and helping them reach this moment. This achievement belongs to you, too. And to the Class of 2026, remember: somewhere in the years ahead, you're going to walk into another room where you have absolutely no idea what you're doing. You've done this before. Go figure it out. And as one MITer to another, I am incredibly honored to be here with you today. Congratulations, Class of 2026.
